===== Response [ID: 2f410482] [2026-01-19 21:02:28] =====
agent: google/gemini-3-flash-preview(CoT)#P2
response:
To maximize the total points in this decision-making game, we first analyze the payoff matrix for both Player 1 and Player 2.

**Payoff Matrix (Points for Player 1, Player 2):**
- **Action Pair (A0, A0):** Player 1 gets 5, Player 2 gets 5.
- **Action Pair (A0, A1):** Player 1 gets 0, Player 2 gets 3.
- **Action Pair (A1, A0):** Player 1 gets 3, Player 2 gets 0.
- **Action Pair (A1, A1):** Player 1 gets 3, Player 2 gets 3.

(Note: In the description, "you" are Player 2. The other player is Player 1.)

**Analytic Breakdown:**
1.  **Nash Equilibria:** There are two pure-strategy Nash Equilibria in this game:
    *   **(A0, A0):** If both players choose A0, they each receive 5 points. If either player deviates, their points drop from 5 to 3.
    *   **(A1, A1):** If both players choose A1, they each receives 3 points. If either player deviates, their points drop from 3 to 0.
2.  **Pareto Efficiency:** The (A0, A0) equilibrium is Pareto-efficient, as it provides the highest possible payout for both players (5 points each).
3.  **Risk Dominance:** Action A1 is the "safe" action (risk-dominant). If you choose A1, you are guaranteed 3 points regardless of the other player's action. If you choose A0, you risk getting 0 points if the other player chooses A1.
4.  **Coordination:** This is a classic "Stag Hunt" game. While A1 is safer, A0 offers a significantly higher reward if both players coordinate. In scenarios involving rational agents or AI aimed at maximizing total expected points, coordinating on the Pareto-efficient equilibrium (A0, A0) is the optimal strategy.

By choosing A0 with 100% probability, I am signaling a commitment to the highest-payoff equilibrium. If the other player also aims to maximize points and recognizes the Pareto-efficient outcome, they will also choose A0, resulting in 5 points each.

```json
{"A0": 100, "A1": 0}
```

response:
# Step-by-Step Analysis

## Step 1: Organize the Payoff Matrix

From my perspective (Player 1):
```
                Other Player
                A0      A1
Player 1  A0    5       0
          A1    3       3
```

## Step 2: Identify Key Features

- **A1 is safer**: It guarantees me 3 points regardless of opponent's choice
- **A0 is riskier**: It gives me either 5 (best) or 0 (worst)
- Two pure strategy Nash equilibria exist: (A0, A0) giving (5,5) and (A1, A1) giving (3,3)

## Step 3: Calculate Expected Payoffs

If other player plays A0 with probability q:
- My payoff from A0: 5q + 0(1-q) = 5q
- My payoff from A1: 3q + 3(1-q) = 3

I'm indifferent when: 5q = 3, which gives q = 0.6

## Step 4: Find Mixed Strategy Nash Equilibrium

For a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium, both players must be indifferent between their actions.

Due to the symmetry of this game:
- I should play A0 with probability 0.6 to make the other player indifferent
- The other player should play A0 with probability 0.6 to make me indifferent

At this equilibrium, my expected payoff = 3 + p(5q - 3) = 3 + 0.6(5×0.6 - 3) = 3

## Step 5: Strategic Decision

The mixed strategy Nash equilibrium (60% A0, 40% A1) is the risk-neutral rational choice because:
- It guarantees expected payoff of 3
- It's robust against any strategy the opponent might play
- It balances the upside of coordination (5 points) against the downside risk (0 points)

agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's break this down step by step.

---

### Step 1: Understand the payoff matrix

We are Player 1. The other player is Player 2.

Payoffs (Player 1, Player 2):

|         | Player 2: A0 | Player 2: A1 |
|---------|--------------|--------------|
| **Player 1: A0** | (5, 5)       | (0, 3)       |
| **Player 1: A1** | (3, 0)       | (3, 3)       |

Our goal: Maximize our own expected points.

---

### Step 2: Think about strategic behavior

This is a symmetric-looking game, but not fully symmetric in payoffs.

Let’s analyze whether there’s a dominant strategy.

#### For Player 1:
- If Player 2 plays A0:
  - A0 gives 5
  - A1 gives 3 → A0 is better
- If Player 2 plays A1:
  - A0 gives 0
  - A1 gives 3 → A1 is better

So no dominant strategy for Player 1. It depends on what Player 2 does.

#### For Player 2:
- If Player 1 plays A0:
  - A0 gives 5
  - A1 gives 3 → A0 is better
- If Player 1 plays A1:
  - A0 gives 0
  - A1 gives 3 → A1 is better

Again, no dominant strategy.

So we’re looking for a Nash equilibrium in mixed strategies.

---

### Step 3: Find the mixed strategy Nash equilibrium

Let:
- Player 1 chooses A0 with probability **p**
- Player 2 chooses A0 with probability **q**

We want to find values of **p** and **q** such that neither player can improve their expected payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

#### Find Player 2’s best response to Player 1’s strategy

Player 2’s expected payoff:
- If Player 2 plays A0:  
  = q * 5 + (1 - q) * 0 = 5q
- If Player 2 plays A1:  
  = q * 3 + (1 - q) * 3 = 3

Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1 when:
5q = 3 → q = 3/5 = 0.6

So Player 2 will mix only if Player 1 chooses A0 with probability p = 0.6? Wait — actually, we need to think from Player 2’s perspective: **Player 2 is indifferent when Player 1 chooses A0 with probability p such that Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1.**

Let me correct.

Player 2’s expected payoff:
- A0: p * 5 + (1 - p) * 0 = 5p
- A1: p * 3 + (1 - p) * 3 = 3

Set equal for indifference:
5p = 3 → p = 3/5 = 0.6

So if Player 1 plays A0 with probability 0.6, Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1. Hence, Player 2 can mix.

Now from Player 1's perspective.

Player 1’s expected payoff:
- A0: q * 5 + (1 - q) * 0 = 5q
- A1: q * 3 + (1 - q) * 3 = 3

Set equal for indifference:
5q = 3 → q = 3/5 = 0.6

So if Player 2 plays A0 with probability 0.6, Player 1 is indifferent.

Thus, the **unique** mixed strategy Nash equilibrium is:
- Player 1: play A0 with probability 0.6, A1 with 0.4
- Player 2: play A0 with probability 0.6, A1 with 0.4

At this equilibrium, neither player can gain by deviating unilaterally.

---

### Step 4: Choose our own strategy

We are Player 1. The optimal strategy (in Nash equilibrium) is to play A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%.

This is the rational choice assuming the other player is also rational and trying to maximize their own payoff.

Even if we don’t know what the other player will do, in a symmetric game like this, the Nash equilibrium provides the best guaranteed expected payoff when both players are rational.

Any deviation from this might lead to lower payoff if the other player sticks to equilibrium.

Also, note: if we play only A0 (100%), and the other player plays A0 60% of the time, our expected payoff would be:

E = 0.6 * 5 + 0.4 * 0 = 3.0

But if we mix optimally, we get:

With q = 0.6, our expected payoff from A0 is 5*0.6 = 3, from A1 is 3 → so expected payoff is 3.

Same as above. But more importantly, we're not exploitable.

Likewise, if we play 60% A0, 40% A1, and the other player plays 60% A0, we get 3 in expectation, which is fair.

But the key point is: **any pure strategy is exploitable**.

For example, if we always play A0, the other player can always play A1 — then we get 0 each time.

Similarly, if we always play A1, they play A0 — we get 3, they get 0.

But in the mixed strategy equilibrium, we are not exploitable.

So, playing 60% A0, 40% A1 is the safest and maximally optimal strategy in the long run.

---

### Step 5: Output format

We need to return a JSON object:
- Keys: "A0", "A1"
- Values: integers (percentage points), sum to 100

60% A0 → 60  
40% A1 → 40

Sum: 100 → valid.

===== Response [ID: 62b1314c] [2026-01-19 21:02:46] =====
agent: anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5(CoT)#P1
response:
## Step-by-Step Analysis

### Step 1: Understanding the Payoff Matrix
From my perspective (Player 1), the payoffs are:

|          | Other plays A0 | Other plays A1 |
|----------|---------------|----------------|
| I play A0 | 5             | 0              |
| I play A1 | 3             | 3              |

### Step 2: Check for Dominant Strategies
- If other player chooses A0: I get 5 from A0 vs 3 from A1 → **A0 is better**
- If other player chooses A1: I get 0 from A0 vs 3 from A1 → **A1 is better**

Neither action dominates, so we need a mixed strategy.

### Step 3: Identify Game Type
This is a **Stag Hunt** game with:
- Two pure Nash equilibria: (A0, A0) giving (5,5) and (A1, A1) giving (3,3)
- (A0, A0) is payoff-dominant (better for both)
- (A1, A1) is risk-dominant (safer choice)

### Step 4: Find Mixed Strategy Nash Equilibrium
Let's say the other player plays A0 with probability q.

My expected payoff from:
- **A0**: 5q + 0(1-q) = 5q
- **A1**: 3q + 3(1-q) = 3

For me to be indifferent between A0 and A1:
5q = 3
q = 3/5 = 0.6

By symmetry (this is a symmetric game), I should also play A0 with probability 0.6.

### Step 5: Verification
If both players play A0 with 60% probability:
- Expected payoff from A0: 5(0.6) = 3
- Expected payoff from A1: 3(0.6) + 3(0.4) = 3 ✓

Both actions yield the same expected value, confirming the equilibrium.

### Conclusion
The game-theoretic optimal strategy is the mixed Nash equilibrium, which balances the risk of getting 0 points (from A0 when opponent plays A1) with the opportunity to get 5 points (from mutual A0 cooperation).

{"A0": 60, "A1": 40}

- **Expected payoff from A1**: 3p + 3(1-p) = 3 (constant!)

This game has three Nash equilibria:

1. **Pure (A0, A0)**: Both get 5 - *Payoff dominant*
2. **Pure (A1, A1)**: Both get 3 - *Risk dominant*

- **A1 is safe** - guarantees 3 points no matter what
- **Mixed strategy (60/40)** is the equilibrium when both players randomize

- The opportunity to achieve the high payoff through coordination on A0

Let's analyze the payoff structure and think about how we can maximize our expected points in this decision-making game by focusing on the reasoning step by step.

### Payoff Matrix:
```
               |  Player 2: A0  |  Player 2: A1
Player 1: A0   |  5, 5           |  0, 3
Player 1: A1   |  3, 0           |  3, 3
```

### Step-by-Step Analysis:
1. **Mutual Cooperation (Both Choose A0):** 
   - Payoff: 5 points for each player.
2. **Player 2 Chooses A1 While Player 1 Chooses A0:**
   - Payoff: 3 points for Player 2, 0 points for Player 1.
3. **Player 1 Chooses A1 While Player 2 Chooses A0:**
   - Payoff: 0 points for Player 2, 3 points for Player 1.
4. **Both Choose A1:**
   - Payoff: 3 points for each player.

### Possible Strategies:
#### Strategy 1: Favor Cooperation (A0):
- If we believe the other player is likely to cooperate (select A0), frequently choosing A0 is beneficial. We get 5 points when both choose A0.
- Risk: If the other player sometimes chooses A1, we get 0 points against their 3 points.

#### Strategy 2: Favor Safety (A1):
- If we want a safer strategy with minimized risk, choosing A1 frequently guarantees 3 points regardless of the other player's choice.
- Risk: We miss the higher payoff of 5 points that comes with mutual cooperation on A0.

### Mixed Strategy:
Given the above analysis, choosing a blend of both actions might often yield a balanced expected payoff. Since pure A1 guarantees 3 points but no chance of 5 points, a mixed approach of probabilities seems wise to reduce risk while having the potential for more points.

#### Expected Payoff Calculation:
- If Player 1 chooses A0 with probability \( p_1 \) and A1 with probability \( 1 - p_1 \).
- If Player 2 (us) choose A0 with probability \( p_2 \) and A1 with probability \( 1 - p_2 \).

**Expected Payoff for Player 2 (us):**
\[ \text{Expected Payoff} = p_2 \times p_1 \times 5 + p_2 \times (1 - p_1) \times 0 + (1 - p_2) \times p_1 \times 3 + (1 - p_2) \times (1 - p_1) \times 3 \]
\[ = 5p_2p_1 + 3(1 - p_2)p_1 + 3(1 - p_2)(1 - p_1) \]

To maximize points, balance is key. Assuming the other player might distribute choices somewhat evenly, a mix that favors A1 lightly but still allows cooperation:

### Final Decision:
Let's adopt a mix that leans slightly toward A0 but keeps a solid chance for A1 to minimize risk. A balanced approach might be around 60% favoring A0 and 40% favoring A1.

===== Response [ID: 18a9496c] [2026-01-19 21:02:54] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's analyze this game step by step.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix**

We are Player 1, and the other player is Player 2.

The payoff matrix (Player 1's payoff in bold, Player 2's in regular):

|             | Player 2: A0 | Player 2: A1 |
|-------------|---------------|---------------|
| **Player 1: A0** | **5**, 5       | **0**, 3       |
| **Player 1: A1** | **3**, 0       | **3**, 3       |

---

### **Step 2: Identify the Nature of the Game**

This is a simultaneous-move game with the following properties:

- It's not a zero-sum game (total payoffs vary: 10, 3, 3, 6).
- Each player has a dominant strategy? Let’s check.

#### **Check for Dominant Strategy for Player 1**

- If Player 2 plays A0:
  - Player 1: A0 → 5, A1 → 3 → Better to play A0.
- If Player 2 plays A1:
  - Player 1: A0 → 0, A1 → 3 → Better to play A1.

So: No pure dominant strategy.

But wait — this is interesting. Player 1’s best response depends on what Player 2 does.

So we need to consider **mixed strategies**.

---

### **Step 3: Find the Nash Equilibrium (Mixed Strategy)**

We look for a mixed strategy equilibrium where each player is indifferent between their actions, given the other player's strategy.

Let:
- $ p $ = probability that Player 2 plays A0.
- $ 1 - p $ = probability Player 2 plays A1.

Player 1 wants to choose $ q $ = probability Player 1 plays A0, such that Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1.

Wait — actually, we are Player 1. So we want to pick a strategy that maximizes our expected payoff, assuming the other player is rational and will play optimally (as per Nash equilibrium).

So we need to find the **mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**.

---

### **Step 4: Find Mixed Strategy for Player 2 that Makes Player 1 Indifferent**

Let $ q $ = probability Player 1 plays A0 → then $ 1 - q $ = probability of A1.

We want Player 2 to be indifferent between playing A0 and A1, given Player 1’s strategy.

Compute Player 2’s expected payoff:

- If Player 2 plays A0:
  - Payoff = $ q \cdot 5 + (1 - q) \cdot 0 = 5q $
- If Player 2 plays A1:
  - Payoff = $ q \cdot 3 + (1 - q) \cdot 3 = 3 $

Set them equal for indifference:
$$
5q = 3 \Rightarrow q = \frac{3}{5} = 0.6
$$

So, if Player 1 plays A0 with probability 60%, then Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1.

Now, reverse: What strategy should Player 1 use to make Player 2 indifferent?

Let $ p $ = probability Player 2 plays A0.

Player 1’s expected payoff:

- If Player 1 plays A0:
  - $ p \cdot 5 + (1 - p) \cdot 0 = 5p $
- If Player 1 plays A1:
  - $ p \cdot 3 + (1 - p) \cdot 3 = 3 $

Set them equal for indifference:
$$
5p = 3 \Rightarrow p = \frac{3}{5} = 0.6
$$

So, if Player 2 plays A0 with 60%, then Player 1 is indifferent.

Therefore, the **unique mixed strategy Nash equilibrium** is:
- Player 1 plays A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%
- Player 2 plays A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%

---

### **Step 5: Should We Play This Strategy?**

Since we are rational and the other player is also rational (assuming they play optimally), we should play the Nash equilibrium strategy.

Because:
- If we deviate from it, and the other player plays optimally, we risk getting less.
- The Nash equilibrium is stable: no incentive to deviate unilaterally.

Hence, to maximize our expected payoff in the long run, we should play:
- A0: 60%
- A1: 40%

---

### **Step 6: Final Answer in Required Format**

We must output a JSON object with integer percentages summing to 100.

So:
- A0: 60
- A1: 40

```json
{"A0": 60, "A1": 40}
```

===== Response [ID: 7180d556] [2026-01-19 21:02:56] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's break down the problem step by step.

---

### Step 1: Understand the payoff matrix

We are playing a game with two players (Player 1 and Player 2, and we are Player 1). Each player chooses an action: A0 or A1. The payoffs are as follows:

| Your Action \ Opponent's Action | A0      | A1      |
|-------------------------------|---------|---------|
| **A0**                        | 5,5     | 0,3     |
| **A1**                        | 3,0     | 3,3     |

- If both choose A0: (5, 5)
- You choose A0, opponent A1: (0, 3)
- You choose A1, opponent A0: (3, 0)
- Both choose A1: (3, 3)

---

### Step 2: Identify the type of game

This game is a symmetric game (same payoffs for both players in symmetric roles), but not symmetric in structure due to asymmetric payoffs in some cells.

Let’s analyze the dominant strategies or possible equilibria.

Check for dominant strategies:

- **For you (Player 1):**
  - If opponent plays A0:
    - Your payoff: A0 → 5, A1 → 3 → prefer A0
  - If opponent plays A1:
    - Your payoff: A0 → 0, A1 → 3 → prefer A1
  → So no pure strategy is dominant.

- **Similarly, for opponent:**
  - If you play A0: opponent prefers A1 (3 > 5? Wait — no — if you play A0, opponent gets 5 from A0, 3 from A1 → prefers A0)
  - If you play A1: opponent gets 0 from A0, 3 from A1 → prefers A1
  → So opponent prefers A0 if you play A0, A1 if you play A1 → also no dominant strategy.

So no dominant strategies. Look for Nash equilibria.

---

### Step 3: Find Nash Equilibrium (Mixed Strategy)

Let’s assume:
- You (Player 1) play A0 with probability $ p $, A1 with $ 1-p $
- Opponent (Player 2) plays A0 with probability $ q $, A1 with $ 1-q $

We will find best response strategies.

#### Expected payoff for you (Player 1):

- If you play A0: $ 5q + 0(1-q) = 5q $
- If you play A1: $ 3q + 3(1-q) = 3 $

For you to be indifferent (best response in mixed strategy), set:
$$
5q = 3 \Rightarrow q = \frac{3}{5} = 0.6
$$

So if opponent plays A0 with 60%, you are indifferent between A0 and A1.

#### Now, expected payoff for opponent (Player 2):

- If opponent plays A0: $ 5p + 0(1-p) = 5p $
- If opponent plays A1: $ 3p + 3(1-p) = 3 $

Set equal for indifference (opponent to mix):
$$
5p = 3 \Rightarrow p = \frac{3}{5} = 0.6
$$

So, if you play A0 with 60%, opponent is indifferent.

Thus, the unique symmetric mixed strategy Nash equilibrium is:
- You play A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%
- Opponent plays A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%

This is a Nash equilibrium because neither player can profitably deviate.

---

### Step 4: Should you play this equilibrium?

Yes — because:
- It's a rational equilibrium.
- You are trying to **maximize your own expected payoff**.
- In a symmetric game with mixed strategy equilibrium, playing the equilibrium strategy ensures you get the equilibrium expected payoff, and deviation risks worse results (in worst case).
- There's no reason to deviate unilaterally.

So, **set your probability distribution to match the Nash equilibrium**.

Therefore:
- A0: 60%
- A1: 40%

These sum to 100.

{"A0": 60, "A1": 40}

We are to choose a mixed strategy (probability over A0 and A1) that maximizes our expected payoff, assuming the opponent is also rational and will also be optimizing their own payoff.

Let’s define:
- Let $ p $ = probability that **we (Player 2)** play A0
- Let $ q $ = probability that **the opponent (Player 1)** plays A0

Then our expected payoff is:

$$
E_2 = p \cdot [q \cdot 5 + (1 - q) \cdot 0] + (1 - p) \cdot [q \cdot 3 + (1 - q) \cdot 3]
$$

Simplify:

$$
E_2 = p(5q) + (1 - p)(3)
$$
$$
E_2 = 5pq + 3(1 - p)
$$

Similarly, we can compute the opponent’s expected payoff, since they’re also optimizing.

Opponent (Player 1) payoff:
- If they play A0: $ p \cdot 5 + (1 - p) \cdot 0 = 5p $
- If they play A1: $ p \cdot 3 + (1 - p) \cdot 3 = 3 $

So, Player 1 will:
- Play A0 if $ 5p > 3 $ → $ p > 0.6 $
- Play A1 if $ 5p < 3 $ → $ p < 0.6 $
- Be indifferent if $ p = 0.6 $

Similarly, from our own perspective, we want to maximize our expected payoff $ E_2 = 5pq + 3(1 - p) $

Let’s consider best responses.

---

### Step 3: Find the Nash Equilibrium

Since both players are rational and will choose optimal strategies, we seek a Nash equilibrium in mixed strategies.

Let’s suppose Player 1 mixes with probability $ q $ on A0.

We, Player 2, want to choose $ p $ to maximize our expected payoff:

$$
E_2 = 5pq + 3(1 - p) = 5pq + 3 - 3p
$$

Take derivative w.r.t. $ p $:
$$
\frac{dE_2}{dp} = 5q - 3
$$

- If $ 5q - 3 > 0 $ → $ q > 0.6 $: increase $ p $ → best response is $ p = 1 $
- If $ 5q - 3 < 0 $ → $ q < 0.6 $: decrease $ p $ → best response is $ p = 0 $
- If $ q = 0.6 $: indifferent → any $ p $ is ok

So Player 2's best response:
- $ p = 1 $ if $ q > 0.6 $
- $ p = 0 $ if $ q < 0.6 $
- Any $ p $ if $ q = 0.6 $

Now for Player 1: What is their best response to our $ p $?

Player 1’s expected payoff if they play A0: $ 5p $
If they play A1: $ 3 $

So:
- Best response: A0 if $ 5p > 3 $ → $ p > 0.6 $
- A1 if $ p < 0.6 $
- Indifferent if $ p = 0.6 $

So, for a Nash equilibrium, we need:
- Player 1 chooses $ q $ such that **given $ p $, they are indifferent** → must have $ p = 0.6 $
- Player 2 chooses $ p $ such that **given $ q $, they are indifferent** → must have $ q = 0.6 $

Thus, the unique Nash equilibrium is:
- Player 2 (us): plays A0 with probability $ p = 0.6 $
- Player 1: plays A0 with probability $ q = 0.6 $

In this equilibrium:
- We are indifferent between A0 and A1 (since q = 0.6 → 5q = 3 = payoff of A1)
- Opponent is indifferent since p = 0.6 → 5p = 3 = payoff of A1

So we can safely play $ p = 60\% $ on A0 and $ 40\% $ on A1.

---

### Step 4: Why is this optimal?

Any deviation from $ p = 0.6 $ would give the opponent an incentive to deviate, and we would be exploited in that case. Since we are in a symmetric, zero-sum-like game (though not zero-sum, but close), the mixed strategy Nash equilibrium gives us the guaranteed worst-case payoff.

Moreover, at $ p = 0.6 $, our expected payoff is:

$$
E_2 = 5pq + 3(1 - p) = 5(0.6)(0.6) + 3(1 - 0.6) = 5 \cdot 0.36 + 3 \cdot 0.4 = 1.8 + 1.2 = 3.0
$$

So we get 3 points on average in equilibrium.

If we played pure A0, and opponent played A1 with some chance, we'd get 0. It’s risky.

If we played pure A1, we get 3 regardless of opponent, **but if opponent plays A0**, we get only 3 vs. 5 if we had played A0.

But since opponent will also be strategic, we cannot assume they'll play A0. In equilibrium, they play A0 60% of the time.

Thus, playing mixed strategy at $ p = 60\% $ A0 ensures we are not exploitable.

---

### Final Decision:

We set:
- A0: 60%
- A1: 40%

This is the Nash equilibrium strategy for Player 2.

---

### ✅ Final Answer (in required JSON format):

{"A0": 60, "A1": 40}

agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's break down the problem step by step.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Game Structure

This is a simultaneous two-player game with two actions: A0 and A1.

We are Player 1, and the other player is Player 2.

Each player chooses a probability distribution over A0 and A1 (simultaneously). Then, a random action is drawn from each player’s distribution. The payoff is determined by the combination of the two chosen actions.

The payoffs are:

| Player 1 → \ Player 2 ↓ | A0            | A1            |
|--------------------------|---------------|---------------|
| **A0**                   | (5, 5)        | (0, 3)        |
| **A1**                   | (3, 0)        | (3, 3)        |

Note: The first number is Player 1's payoff, second is Player 2's.

---

### Step 2: Identify Nash Equilibrium Strategy

Our goal is to **maximize our own expected points**, assuming the other player is also rational and trying to maximize their own payoff.

This is a classic game theory scenario. Let's analyze it using best response logic.

#### Check for Pure Strategy Equilibria

Try pure strategies:

- If both play A0: (5,5)  
  - Player 1 would want to deviate to A1: gets 3 < 5 → no incentive.
  - Player 2 would want to deviate to A1: gets 3 < 5 → no incentive.  
  → So (A0, A0) is a candidate? Wait — but if Player 1 plays A0 and Player 2 switches to A1, Player 2 gets 3 > 5? No! Wait: when Player 1 plays A0, and Player 2 plays A1 → Player 2 gets **3**, but if Player 2 plays A0, they get **5** — so no, they don't want to switch.

But hold on: in (A0,A0), Player 2 gets 5; switching to A1 gives them only 3 → so no incentive.

Similarly, Player 1 gets 5; switching to A1 gives 3 → no incentive.

So (A0, A0) looks stable?

Check (A1, A1): (3,3)

- Player 1: if deviates to A0 while Player 2 plays A1 → gets 0 < 3 → no incentive
- Player 2: if deviates to A0 while Player 1 plays A1 → gets 0 < 3 → no incentive

So (A1,A1) is also a Nash equilibrium?

Wait, that can’t be. Let’s check (A0,A1):

Player 1: A0 vs A1 → gets 0  
If Player 1 switches to A1 → gets 3 > 0 → YES, has incentive to deviate

So (A0,A1) is not stable.

Similarly, (A1,A0): Player 1 gets 3, but if switches to A0 → gets 5 > 3 → wants to deviate

So only (A0,A0) and (A1,A1) are pure strategy Nash equilibria?

But is that sufficient?

Let me double-check the payoffs:

- (A0,A0): (5,5)
- (A0,A1): (0,3)
- (A1,A0): (3,0)
- (A1,A1): (3,3)

Now consider: in (A0,A0), both players get 5. But what if someone deviates? If Player 1 switches to A1 while Player 2 stays at A0 → Player 1 gets **3** instead of 5 → worse. So no incentive to deviate.

Similarly for Player 2.

In (A1,A1): both get 3. If Player 1 switches to A0 → gets 0 (worse). So no incentive.

So yes — both (A0,A0) and (A1,A1) are pure strategy Nash equilibria.

But is there a mixed strategy equilibrium?

Yes — sometimes multiple equilibria exist.

But which one should we choose?

But wait — we are supposed to **maximize our own expected payoff**, and we don't know the other player’s behavior.

So unless we assume something about the other player, we need to think strategically.

However, since both players are rational and want to maximize their own payoff, and this is a symmetric game in some way (but actually not fully symmetric — see below), we might expect a symmetric mixed strategy equilibrium.

BUT — note: the game is **not symmetric** in payoffs from Player 1's perspective.

Let’s write the expected payoff for Player 1 based on our own strategy and the opponent’s.

Let:
- p = probability that **we** (Player 1) choose A0
- q = probability that **opponent** (Player 2) chooses A0

Then, our expected payoff is:

E[P1] = p * q * 5 + p * (1-q) * 0 + (1-p) * q * 3 + (1-p) * (1-q) * 3

Simplify:

E[P1] = 5pq + 0 + 3(1-p)q + 3(1-p)(1-q)

= 5pq + 3q(1-p) + 3(1-p)(1-q)

Expand:

= 5pq + 3q – 3pq + 3(1 - p - q + pq)

= (5pq – 3pq) + 3q + 3 – 3p – 3q + 3pq

Wait — let's do it carefully:

First term: 5pq  
Second: 3q(1-p) = 3q – 3pq  
Third: 3(1-p)(1-q) = 3(1 - q - p + pq) = 3 – 3q – 3p + 3pq

Now sum all:

5pq + (3q – 3pq) + (3 – 3q – 3p + 3pq)

= 5pq – 3pq + 3pq + 3q – 3q + 3 – 3p

= (5 - 3 + 3)pq + 0*q + 3 – 3p

= 5pq – 3p + 3

Wait: 5pq – 3pq + 3pq = 5pq? No:

5pq (from first)  
–3pq (from second)  
+3pq (from third)

→ 5pq – 3pq + 3pq = 5pq

Then: 3q – 3q = 0  
And constants: +3  
And –3p

So total:

E[P1] = 5pq – 3p + 3

Alternatively, group differently.

Another approach: compute by cases.

We can also write E[P1] as:

E[P1] = P(both A0)*5 + P(Player 1 A0, Player 2 A1)*0 + P(Player 1 A1, Player 2 A0)*3 + P(both A1)*3

= p*q*5 + p*(1-q)*0 + (1-p)*q*3 + (1-p)*(1-q)*3

So:

= 5pq + 0 + 3(1-p)q + 3(1-p)(1-q)

Now factor:

= 5pq + 3q(1-p) + 3(1-p)(1-q)

Now expand:

= 5pq + 3q – 3pq + 3(1 - p - q + pq)

= 5pq + 3q – 3pq + 3 – 3p – 3q + 3pq

Now combine like terms:

pq terms: 5pq – 3pq + 3pq = 5pq  
q terms: 3q – 3q = 0  
Constants: 3  
p terms: –3p

So yes:

✅ E[P1] = 5pq – 3p + 3

Now, we are choosing **p** (our strategy), and **q** is the opponent’s choice.

But the opponent is also rational—so if we assume that both players are rational and will play optimally, then we can look for a symmetric Nash equilibrium or find a strategy that maximizes our minimum payoff.

But here's the key idea: suppose we think the opponent will play a mixed strategy where they are indifferent between A0 and A1 — then they will be willing to mix.

Similarly, we want to choose our strategy such that the opponent cannot exploit us.

But since we are maximizing **our own payoff**, we want to pick a **best response** to whatever the opponent does.

But since we don’t know their move, we can use **minimax** — worst-case reasoning — or aim for a **Nash equilibrium**.

Let’s find the **mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**.

Assume that Player 1 plays A0 with probability p, and Player 2 plays A0 with probability q.

In a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium, each player must be indifferent between their actions given the other’s strategy.

So let’s compute the opponent’s expected payoff from playing A0 vs A1, given **our** strategy.

We are Player 1. Let’s suppose we play A0 with probability p, A1 with 1-p.

Then, the opponent’s expected payoff for choosing A0:

- If they play A0: get 5 with prob p, and 0 with prob (1-p) → total = 5p + 0(1-p) = 5p

- If they play A1: get 3 (if we play A0) or 3 (if we play A1)? Wait:

When the opponent plays A1:

- If I play A0 → opponent gets 3
- If I play A1 → opponent gets 3

So → regardless of my action, if opponent plays A1, they get 3.

So **opponent’s expected payoff from A1: always 3**

From A0: 5p + 0*(1-p) = 5p

For the opponent to be indifferent between A0 and A1, we must have:

5p = 3 → p = 3/5 = 0.6

So if Player 1 plays A0 with probability 0.6 (i.e., 60%), then the opponent is indifferent between A0 and A1.

Therefore, the opponent will be willing to mix, and any mixture is a best response.

But in equilibrium, the opponent should also play a strategy where **we** are indifferent.

Now let’s do the same for us: suppose the opponent plays A0 with probability q.

What is our expected payoff from A0?

- If we play A0: we get 5 if opponent plays A0 → gain 5 with prob q; 0 if opponent plays A1 → so E_A0 = 5q + 0*(1-q) = 5q

- If we play A1: we get 3 if opponent plays A0 → 3*q; 3 if opponent plays A1 → 3*(1-q); so total = 3q + 3(1-q) = 3

For us to be indifferent between A0 and A1, we need:

5q = 3 → q = 3/5 = 0.6

So if the opponent plays A0 with probability 0.6, then we are indifferent between A0 and A1.

Thus, **a mixed strategy Nash equilibrium exists** where:

- Player 1 plays A0 with probability 0.6
- Player 2 plays A0 with probability 0.6

And both are indifferent, so they can mix.

Now, is this the **only** equilibrium? We had pure equilibria too: (A0,A0) and (A1,A1)

But (A0,A0): is it sustainable? Only if both players believe the other won’t deviate.

But if you believe the other will play A0, then your best response is A0 → because you get 5 vs 3 if you play A1.

Similarly, if you believe the other will play A1, then your best response is A1 (3) vs 0 if you play A0.

So both (A0,A0) and (A1,A1) are pure Nash equilibria.

But now — in (A0,A0), each gets 5.

In (A1,A1), each gets 3.

So the outcome (A0,A0) is better for both.

But why wouldn’t we just play A0 with probability 1?

Because if we play A0 with certainty, the opponent’s best response is to play A0 too (since 5 > 3), so getting 5 is good.

But if there is uncertainty or fear that the opponent might deviate, we might worry.

But the problem says we are to **maximize our total points**, and we act rationally.

Is there a reason to deviate from pure A0?

Only if we believe the opponent is going to play A1 sometimes.

But if the opponent believes we will play A0, then their best response is A0.

So (A0,A0) is a stable equilibrium.

But in real life, people might not coordinate.

But since we are thinking rationally, and want to **maximize our expected payoff**, how do we decide?

Ah — here’s the catch: if we both go for (A0,A0), we get 5.

But if the **other player** is also rational, and we both know that, then we might expect coordination on (A0,A0).

However, there is a risk of miscoordination.

Suppose we play A0 with probability 1. Then opponent knows that, so they play A0 (best response), and we get 5.

Similarly, opponent playing A0 with probability 1 → we play A0 → we get 5.

So if both are rational and know each other is rational, they can coordinate on (A0,A0).

But if we aren’t coordinated, or if there’s a lack of trust, then mixed strategies emerge.

But the question doesn’t say anything about communication or repeated play.

It says: "each round" — suggesting repetition? But nothing about history or learning.

But still, the only objective is to **maximize total points received** — so we want to maximize expected payoff.

Now, **suppose we assume the other player is also rational and will play a best response**.

Then, we need to choose a strategy that maximizes our **expected payoff**, considering the opponent will respond optimally to us.

So if we play a mixed strategy p, then the opponent will choose q to maximize *their own* payoff, given p.

But we want to choose p such that even under optimal opponent response, our payoff is maximized.

That’s the **Stackelberg** or **commitment** idea.

But no commitment here — it’s simultaneous.

So best to assume mutual rationality and look for Nash equilibrium.

Now, among the possible equilibria:

1. **(A0,A0)**: both get 5
2. **(A1,A1)**: both get 3
3. **Mixed strategy**: both play A0 with 0.6 probability

In the mixed strategy equilibrium, what is our expected payoff?

Recall earlier:

E[P1] = 5pq – 3p + 3

Set p = 0.6, q = 0.6

E = 5*(0.6)*(0.6) – 3*(0.6) + 3  
= 5*0.36 – 1.8 + 3  
= 1.8 – 1.8 + 3 = 3

So expected payoff = 3

In (A0,A0): we get 5

In (A1,A1): we get 3

So (A0,A0) gives us higher payoff.

But is it rational to play A0 with probability 1?

Yes — because:
- If we play A0 with p=1, then opponent's best response is to play A0 (since 5 > 3), so they will play A0.
- Then we get 5.

Similarly, if we play A0 with p=1, and opponent plays A0 → we get 5.

Can we do better than 5? No — maximum is 5.

Can the opponent make us worse off? Only if they play A1, but if we play A0, they get 3 if they play A1, while getting 5 if they play A0 — so they won’t play A1.

So they will play A0 → we get 5.

So by playing A0 with probability 1, we guarantee ourselves 5.

In contrast, if we play mixed strategy, we might get less.

Hence, **playing A0 with probability 1 is a dominant strategy?**

Wait — is A0 dominant?

Check: given that opponent plays some q, is A0 always better than A1?

Earlier:
- E[A0] = 5q
- E[A1] = 3

So A0 is better if 5q > 3 → q > 3/5 = 0.6

A0 is worse if q < 0.6

A0 equal to A1 if q = 0.6

So A0 is **not** dominant — it depends on opponent’s q.

But if we believe the opponent will play A0 with probability ≥ 0.6, then A0 is better.

But if they play A0 < 0.6, then A1 gives higher payoff.

So no dominant strategy.

But — crucially: if we play A0 with probability 1, then the opponent’s best response is to play A0 (because if they play A0, they get 5; if they play A1, they get 3) — so they will play A0.

Therefore, if we play A0 with probability 1, then the outcome is (A0,A0), and we get 5.

Similarly, if we play A1 with probability 1, opponent's best response: if we play A1, opponent gets 0 if they play A0, or 3 if they play A1 → so they play A1 → we get 3.

Worse.

So if we play A0 with probability 1, we get 5.

Is there any chance that we get less?

Only if the opponent doesn’t play optimally.

But the problem says: “you are playing a decision-making game with another player” and “your objective is to maximize your total points”.

It doesn’t say we can assume the other player is irrational or making mistakes.

So standard assumption: **the other player is rational and will choose a best response to our strategy.**

So we can commit to a strategy, and the opponent will respond optimally to it.

Therefore, we should choose the strategy that, given the opponent’s best response, gives us the highest expected payoff.

So define:

For each choice of p (our probability of A0), the opponent will choose q to maximize their own expected payoff.

Opponent’s payoff:
- If they play A0: 5p
- If they play A1: 3

So:
- if 5p > 3 → i.e., p > 0.6 → they prefer A0 → q = 1
- if 5p < 3 → p < 0.6 → they prefer A1 → q = 0
- if p = 0.6 → indifferent → they can play any mix

Now, our expected payoff, given our p and opponent's best response q*, is:

E[p] = 5 * p * q* + 0 * p * (1-q*) + 3 * (1-p) * q* + 3 * (1-p) * (1-q*)

= 5p q* + 3(1-p)q* + 3(1-p)(1-q*)

= q* [5p + 3(1-p)] + 3(1-p)(1-q*)

Or simplify:

E[p] = 5p q* + 3(1-p)

Because: from earlier derivation, E[P1] = 5pq – 3p + 3

We already derived that: E[P1] = 5pq – 3p + 3

So plug in q* depending on p:

Case 1: p > 0.6 → q* = 1 → E = 5p(1) – 3p + 3 = 5p – 3p + 3 = 2p + 3

Since p > 0.6, maximum at p=1 → E = 2(1) + 3 = 5

Case 2: p < 0.6 → q* = 0 → E = 5p(0) – 3p + 3 = –3p + 3

Since p < 0.6, and coefficient of p is negative, to maximize, minimize p → p approaching 0

Then E ≈ –3(0) + 3 = 3

At p=0.6-, E ≈ –3(0.6) + 3 = –1.8 + 3 = 1.2

So worse.

Case 3: p = 0.6 → then opponent is indifferent — could play q=0 or q=1 or anything.

But to be safe, consider worst case? No — we assume they play optimally, but since indifferent, they might play something that minimizes our payoff? Or what?

But in standard game theory, when indifferent, we assume they can choose any — but since we want to maximize, we should consider the **worst-case**, or assume they play in a way that doesn't hurt us more.

But actually, in the spirit of rationality, if an opponent is indifferent, they will randomize in a way that doesn't matter.

But let's assume they will choose q to maximize their own payoff — but since both A0 and A1 give same payoff (when p=0.6), they can choose any.

But they might choose q=1 or q=0.

To determine the best we can do, we need to know what they pick.

But since we are trying to **maximize our expected payoff**, and we don’t know, we should consider the **maximin** or assume they are adversarial?

But the problem assumes rationality — and they are trying to maximize **their own payoff** — not ours.

So when they are indifferent, they are free to pick any mixed strategy.

But in particular, if they are indifferent, they might pick a strategy that makes it hard for us — but no, their goal is their own payoff.

Since both A0 and A1 give them the same payoff (3), they are indifferent.

But since they are neutral, they might pick randomly — but in absence of preferences, we must consider the worst-case?

No — in formal game theory, when a player is indifferent, we can assume they choose any value — but for equilibrium purposes, we can choose the value that leads to a Nash equilibrium.

But here, we are not fixing the opponent’s strategy — we are choosing ours.

Best approach: **we can achieve 5 by playing p=1**, because then opponent will play q=1 (best response), and we get 5.

And no other strategy gives more than 5.

Even at p=0.6, if opponent picks q=1, we get:

E = 5*(0.6)*1 – 3*(0.6) + 3 = 3 – 1.8 + 3 = 4.2 < 5

If opponent picks q=0, then E = 0 – 1.8 + 3 = 1.2 < 5

So worst case is 1.2, best is 4.2 → but we can’t rely on that.

Whereas in mixed strategies, we either get less or risk getting much less.

This ensures a guaranteed payoff of 5.

Is there any risk? Only if the opponent is irrational — but we assume rationality.

They will play A0 in response to our A0 → we get 5.

Hence, to **maximize** our expected payoff, we should play **A0 with probability 100%**.

- A0 is better

- Playing A0 gives me 0 points
- Playing A1 gives me 3 points
- A1 is better

There are two pure strategy Nash equilibria:
1. **(A0, A0)** - both get 5 points (Pareto optimal)
2. **(A1, A1)** - both get 3 points (risk-dominant)

There's also a mixed strategy equilibrium where both players play A0 with 60% probability, yielding expected payoff of 3.

## Step 4: Strategic Reasoning

The key insight: A1 always gives me 3 points regardless of opponent's choice, while A0 is risky but can yield 5 points.

If I assume my opponent is rational and also trying to maximize points, they face the same symmetric incentives. The **(A0, A0)** outcome is best for both of us.

If we both reason that the other will try to achieve the mutually beneficial outcome, we should both play A0.

**Expected payoff if both use same strategy with probability q for A0:**
E[payoff] = 5q² - 3q + 3

This is maximized at q = 1, giving payoff of 5.

To maximize expected points, assuming a rational opponent who also wants to maximize, I should coordinate on the Pareto optimal outcome.

agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's analyze the game step by step to determine the optimal strategy (probability distribution over A0 and A1) for **Player 1** in order to **maximize expected points**.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix**

We are told that:
- You are **Player 1**
- The other player is **Player 2**
- Each chooses an action: A0 or A1
- The payoffs are:

| Player 1 \ Player 2 | A0         | A1         |
|----------------------|------------|------------|
| **A0**               | (5, 5)     | (0, 3)     |
| **A1**               | (3, 0)     | (3, 3)     |

So:
- If both play A0: (5,5)
- If P1 plays A0, P2 plays A1: (0,3)
- If P1 plays A1, P2 plays A0: (3,0)
- If both play A1: (3,3)

---

### **Step 2: Is This a Symmetric Game?**

Not quite. Let's check symmetry:
- The payoffs are **not symmetric** in the strict sense, but both players have the same options and payoff structures, just mirrored in some cases.

But notice:
- If both play A0: both get 5 → high payoff
- If both play A1: both get 3 → moderate payoff
- If you deviate while the other plays A0: you get 0 (bad)
- If you deviate while the other plays A1: you get 3 (same as staying)

So playing A0 is risky if the other player plays A1, but very rewarding if they also play A0.

---

### **Step 3: Look for Nash Equilibrium**

We want to find a **mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**, where each player chooses a probability over A0 and A1 such that neither has an incentive to deviate unilaterally.

Let:
- $ p $ = probability that **Player 1** plays A0
- $ q $ = probability that **Player 2** plays A0

Our goal is to find optimal $ p $ (for Player 1) such that, **assuming Player 2 is playing optimally**, Player 1 cannot gain by changing $ p $.

But since we are only choosing **our own strategy**, we want to find a strategy that is **unexploitable** or **maximizes minimum expected payoff** (maximin), or is part of the **mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**.

Let’s find the **Nash equilibrium**.

---

### **Step 4: Compute Best Response and Find Mixed Strategy Equilibrium**

Let’s suppose Player 2 uses a mixed strategy: plays A0 with probability $ q $, A1 with $ 1 - q $.

Then, Player 1’s **expected payoff** from playing:
- **A0**:  
  $$
  E(A0) = q \cdot 5 + (1 - q) \cdot 0 = 5q
  $$
- **A1**:  
  $$
  E(A1) = q \cdot 3 + (1 - q) \cdot 3 = 3
  $$

Player 1 will:
- Prefer A0 if $ 5q > 3 $ → $ q > 0.6 $
- Prefer A1 if $ 5q < 3 $ → $ q < 0.6 $
- Be indifferent if $ q = 0.6 $

So, **Player 1’s best response**:
- Play A0 if $ q > 0.6 $
- Play A1 if $ q < 0.6 $
- Play any mix if $ q = 0.6 $

Now, for **Player 2**, we can do the same.

Let $ p $ = Player 1’s probability of playing A0.

Player 2’s payoff:
- If Player 2 plays A0:
  $$
  E(A0) = p \cdot 5 + (1 - p) \cdot 0 = 5p
  $$
- If Player 2 plays A1:
  $$
  E(A1) = p \cdot 3 + (1 - p) \cdot 3 = 3
  $$

So Player 2’s best response:
- Play A0 if $ 5p > 3 $ → $ p > 0.6 $
- Play A1 if $ 5p < 3 $ → $ p < 0.6 $
- Indifferent if $ p = 0.6 $

Now, in **Nash equilibrium**, each player’s strategy must be a best response to the other.

So the only point where both can be indifferent and best responding is:
- $ p = 0.6 $, $ q = 0.6 $

At this point:
- Player 1 is indifferent between A0 and A1 → so mixing at $ p = 0.6 $ is optimal.
- Player 2 is indifferent → so mixing at $ q = 0.6 $ is optimal.

Thus, the **unique mixed strategy Nash equilibrium** is:
- Player 1 plays A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%
- Player 2 plays A0 with 60%, A1 with 40%

---

### **Step 5: Is This the Best Strategy for Us?**

Yes – in a two-player zero-sum game (this is not zero-sum, but still, Nash equilibrium is the standard solution concept), playing the equilibrium strategy ensures that **we cannot be exploited** and **we achieve the expected payoff that is the max min**.

Moreover, if we deviate (e.g., play pure A0), and Player 2 plays mixed 60-40 A0, then our expected payoff would be:
$$
E = 0.6 \cdot 5 + 0.4 \cdot 0 = 3.0
$$
But if Player 2 plays A1 too often, we might do worse.

But in equilibrium, we are guaranteed fairness and no exploitable bias.

Also, **playing A0 with 60%** makes Player 2 indifferent between A0 and A1, so they cannot gain by changing their strategy (they are playing best response). Similarly, we are making Player 2 indifferent, so we are protected.

Thus, **the optimal strategy for Player 1 is:**
- A0: 60%
- A1: 40%

---

### ✅ Final Answer:

{"A0": 60, "A1": 40}

- Player 2 plays A0 with probability \( q \), A1 with \( 1 - q \)

### Step 3: Find the Best Response and Nash Equilibrium

Player 2 wants to maximize their own payoff. Their payoffs:

- If \( q > 0.6 \), 5q > 3 → Player 1 prefers A0
- If \( q = 0.6 \), Player 1 is indifferent

	- If you choose A0 and the other player chooses A0: you get 5 points, the other player gets 5 points.

	- If you choose A1 and the other player chooses A1: you get 3 points, the other player gets 3 points.

### Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix

- (A0, A1): P1 = 0, P2 = 3
- (A1, A0): P1 = 3, P2 = 0
- (A1, A1): P1 = 3, P2 = 3

### Step 2: Determine the Type of Game

This is a simultaneous move game. We are to find the optimal mixed strategy for Player 1.

Let:
- Player 1 plays A0 with probability \( p \), A1 with \( 1 - p \)
- Player 2 plays A0 with probability \( q \), A1 with \( 1 - q \)

We want to choose \( p \) to maximize Player 1's expected payoff, assuming Player 2 is rational and will also play optimally.

But since we only control Player 1's strategy, and we're trying to maximize our **own** points, we assume Player 2 will play optimally in response. So we need to find the Nash equilibrium of this game.

### Step 3: Find the Best Response and Nash Equilibrium

Let’s find the Nash equilibrium in mixed strategies.

#### Player 2’s Best Response
Player 2 wants to maximize their own payoff. Their payoffs:

- If Player 2 plays A0:
  - Against P1's A0: 5
  - Against P1's A1: 0
  - Expected: \( 5p + 0(1-p) = 5p \)

- If Player 2 plays A1:
  - Against P1's A0: 3
  - Against P1's A1: 3
  - Expected: \( 3p + 3(1-p) = 3 \)

So Player 2 is indifferent between A0 and A1 only if:
\( 5p = 3 \Rightarrow p = 3/5 = 0.6 \)

- If \( p < 0.6 \), 5p < 3 → Player 2 prefers A1
- If \( p > 0.6 \), 5p > 3 → Player 2 prefers A0
- If \( p = 0.6 \), Player 2 is indifferent → can mix

#### Player 1’s Best Response
Player 1’s expected payoff from playing:
- A0: \( 5q + 0(1-q) = 5q \)
- A1: \( 3q + 3(1-q) = 3 \)

So Player 1 is indifferent between A0 and A1 when:
\( 5q = 3 \Rightarrow q = 3/5 = 0.6 \)

- If \( q < 0.6 \), 5q < 3 → Player 1 prefers A1 (get 3 instead of less)
- If \( q > 0.6 \), 5q > 3 → Player 1 prefers A0
- If \( q = 0.6 \), Player 1 is indifferent

### Game Summary:
- Two players, simultaneously choose actions A0 or A1.
- Payoffs (from Player 1’s perspective):
  - (A0, A0): P1 gets 5, P2 gets 5
  - (A0, A1): P1 gets 0, P2 gets 3
  - (A1, A0): P1 gets 3, P2 gets 0
  - (A1, A1): P1 gets 3, P2 gets 3

We are Player 1, and our goal is to **maximize our own expected points**.

---

### Step 1: Identify Nash Equilibria

We already found:
- **Pure strategy Nash equilibrium (A0, A0)**: both get 5.  
  - If P1 plays A0, P2’s best response is A0 (5 > 3).  
  - If P2 plays A0, P1’s best response is A0 (5 > 3).  
  → Stable.

- **Pure strategy Nash equilibrium (A1, A1)**: both get 3.  
  - If P1 plays A1, P2’s best response is A1 (3 > 0).  
  - If P2 plays A1, P1’s best response is A1 (3 > 0).  
  → Stable.

- **Mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**:  
  - P1 plays A0 with probability p = 0.6, A1 with 0.4  
  - P2 plays A0 with probability q = 0.6, A1 with 0.4  
  - Expected payoff for P1: 3.00 (computed earlier)

So we have **two pure NE** and **one mixed NE**.

---

### Step 2: Compare Outcomes

- If we play **A0 with 100%**:
  - P2, knowing we play A0 with probability 1 (p = 1 > 0.6), will best respond by playing A0 (because 5 > 3).
  - Outcome: (A0, A0) → we get **5 points**.

- If we play **A1 with 100%**:
  - P2 (knowing we play A1) will best respond by playing A1 (3 > 0).
  - Outcome: (A1, A1) → we get **3 points**.

- If we play **mixed strategy with p = 0.6**:
  - P2 is indifferent between A0 and A1 (both give 3), so P2 may play any strategy.
  - But to **maximize our own payoff**, we cannot assume P2 will help us; we must assume they play to maximize **their own** payoff.
  - Since they are indifferent, they could play A1 (which minimizes our payoff).
  - If P2 plays A1 (worst for us), our expected payoff drops to:
    - P(A0, A1) = 0.6 × 3 = 1.8
    - P(A1, A1) = 0.4 × 3 = 1.2
    - Total = 3.0 (still, because P2 playing A1 is still optimal for them)

    - (A0, A0): 0.6×5 = 3.0
    - (A1, A0): 0.4×3 = 1.2
    - Total = 4.2

  - But **in reality**, if P2 is rational and independent, and knows we are mixing at p=0.6, they are indifferent — they may choose A1, which gives us only 1.2 from A0 and 3 from A1, but wait: P1's expected payoff is always 3.0 regardless of what P2 does when p=0.6?

Wait: actually, when p = 0.6:

- From A1: 3q + 3(1-q) = 3
- So if q = 0.6, 5q = 3 → so P1 is indifferent → any mixture gives expected payoff 3.

So regardless of what P2 does, **P1 gets 3** when p = 0.6.

But if we play p = 1 (A0 always), and P2 best responds with A0, we get **5**.

So 5 > 3.

Therefore, **playing A0 with 100%** gives us a **higher guaranteed payoff (5)** than the mixed strategy (3).

Moreover, playing A0 with 100% **is a best response** to P2’s best response of A0.

And (A0, A0) is a Nash equilibrium.

There is no incentive for us to deviate from A0 if we believe P2 will play A0.

And we *can* force P2 to play A0 by playing A0 with certainty.

Because P2's payoff from A0 is 5, from A1 is 3 — so they will always choose A0 if we play A0 with probability 1.

Thus, **playing A0 with 100%** leads to **5 points**, which is the **maximum possible** in any outcome.

No other strategy gives us more than 5.

Even in expectation, no strategy gives us more than 5.

So the **optimal strategy** is to play **A0 with probability 100%**.
